2:21:1125:0:14:71:486:708:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:!:Rotten Apples: An Investigation of the Prevalence and Predictors of Teacher Cheating
2:22:1209:14:14:71:245:694:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:!:Brian A. Jacob and Steven D. Levitt
2:23:1244:14:14:71:227:680:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:!:NBER Working Paper No. 9413
2:24:1271:14:14:71:147:666:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:!:December 2002
2:25:1284:14:28:71:160:652:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:!:JEL No. I20, K42 
2:26:1301:28:21:271:339:624:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:!:ABSTRACT 
2:27:1310:21:22:107:539:603:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:!:We  develop  an  algorithm  for  detecting  teacher  cheating  that  combines  information  on
2:28:1404:22:21:71:539:581:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:!:unexpected test score fluctuations and suspicious patterns of answers for students in a classroom.
2:29:1502:21:22:71:539:560:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:!:Using  data  from  the  Chicago  Public  Schools,  we  estimate  that  serious  cases  of  teacher  or
2:30:1604:22:21:71:539:538:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:!:administrator cheating on standardized tests occur in a minimum of 4-5 percent of elementary school
2:31:1703:21:22:71:540:517:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:!:classrooms annually. Moreover, the observed frequency of cheating appears to respond strongly to
2:32:1799:22:22:71:540:495:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:!:relatively  minor  changes  in  incentives.  Our  results  highlight  the  fact  that  incentive  systems,
2:33:1905:22:21:71:540:473:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:!:especially  those  with  bright  line  rules,  often  induce  behavioral  distortions  such  as  cheating.
2:34:2011:21:22:71:539:452:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:!:Statistical analysis, however, may provide a means of detecting illicit acts, despite the best attempts
2:35:2114:22:53:71:266:430:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:!:of perpetrators to keep them clandestine.
2:36:2155:53:17:71:387:377:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:!:Brian JacobSteven Levitt
2:37:2179:17:17:71:449:360:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:!:Kennedy School of GovernmentDepartment of Economics

2:42:2356:17:0:71:469:275:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:!:brian_jacob@harvard,eduslevitt@midway.uchicago.edu
3:43:2408:0:27:71:150:708:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:C:I. Introduction 
3:44:2424:27:28:107:516:681:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:A:High-stakes testing has become an increasingly prominent feature of the educational 
3:45:2508:28:27:71:490:653:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:landscape.  Every state in the country except for Iowa currently administers state-wide 
3:46:2596:27:28:71:518:626:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:assessment tests to students in elementary and secondary school.  Twenty-four states require 
3:47:2689:28:28:71:528:598:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:students to pass an exit examination to graduate high school.  Twenty states reward schools on 
3:48:2784:28:27:71:524:570:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:the basis of exemplary or improved student performance on standardized exams and 32 states 
3:49:2875:27:28:71:503:543:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:sanction schools on the basis of poor student performance on these exams.  In the state of 
3:50:2966:28:27:71:543:515:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:California, a policy providing for merit pay bonuses of as much as $25,000 per teacher in schools 
3:51:3064:27:28:71:521:488:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:with large test score gains was recently put into place.  Recent federal legislation promises to 
3:52:3161:28:27:71:509:460:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:accelerate this trend.  The reauthorization of the Elementary and Secondary Education Act 
3:53:3251:27:28:71:529:433:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:(ESEA) requires states to test students in third through eighth grade each year and to judge the 
3:54:3348:28:28:71:375:405:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:performance of schools based on student achievement scores.   
3:55:3410:28:27:107:542:377:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:A:Proponents of high-stakes testing argue that requiring students to demonstrate proficiency 
3:56:3501:27:28:71:508:350:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:in basic skills provides increased incentives for learning, as well as preventing unqualified 
3:57:3595:28:27:71:520:322:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:students from being promoted to higher-level grades where their inadequate preparation may 
3:58:3686:27:28:71:538:295:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:interfere with other students’ learning.  By linking teacher salary and employment to student test 
3:59:3785:28:28:71:526:267:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:scores, schools are held accountable for their students’ performance.  Opponents of test-based 
3:60:3880:28:27:71:529:239:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:accountability, on the other hand, argue that linking incentives to performance on standardized 
3:61:3976:27:28:71:533:212:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:tests may unfairly penalize certain students and will lead teachers to substitute away from other 
3:62:4074:28:27:71:537:184:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:teaching skills or topics not directly tested on the accountability exam (Holmstrom and Milgrom 
3:63:4170:27:0:71:500:157:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:1991).  Studies of districts that have implemented such policies provide mixed evidence, 
4:64:4261:0:27:71:510:708:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:suggesting some improvements in student performance along with indications of increased 
4:65:4349:27:28:71:360:681:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:teaching to the test and shifts away from non-tested areas.1  
4:66:4411:28:27:107:542:653:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:A:In this paper, we explore a very different concern regarding high-stakes testing—cheating 
4:67:4501:27:472:71:445:626:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:on the part of teachers and administrators.2  As incentives for high test scores 
4:68:4582:472:7:215:218:154:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:!:                                                           
4:69:4640:479:16:71:525:147:42:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:1 See, for example, Deere and Strayer (2001), Grissmer et. al. (2000), Heubert and Hauser (1999), Jacob (2001a, 
4:70:4752:16:7:71:495:131:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:2001b), Klein et. al. (2000),Richards and Sheu (1992), Smith and Mickelson (2000), and  Tepper (2001). 
4:71:4855:7:0:71:539:124:42:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:*:2  Hereafter, we uses the phrase “teacher cheating” to encompass cheating done by either teachers or administrators. 
4:72:4971:0:28:71:512:598:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:increase, unscrupulous teachers may be more likely to engage in a range of illicit activities, 
4:73:5066:28:27:71:537:570:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:including changing student responses on answer sheets, filling in the blanks when a student fails 
4:74:5164:27:28:71:532:543:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:to complete a section, allowing students extra time to complete tests, providing correct answers 
4:75:5261:28:27:71:511:515:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:to students, or obtaining copies of an exam illegitimately prior to the test date and teaching 
4:76:5356:27:28:71:530:488:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:students using knowledge of the precise exam questions.  While such allegations may seem far-
4:77:5449:28:27:71:523:460:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:fetched, documented cases of such cheating have recently been uncovered in California (May 
4:78:5540:27:28:71:502:433:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:2000), Massachusetts (Marcus 2000), New York (Loughran and Comiskey  1999), Texas 
4:79:5622:28:28:71:383:405:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:(Kolker 1999), and Great Britain (Hofkins 1995, Tysome 1994). 
4:80:5684:28:27:107:513:377:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:A:Although the absolute number of teachers and administrators who have been caught 
4:81:5765:27:28:71:523:350:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:cheating to date is very small, there are indications that the prevalence of cheating may be far 
4:82:5862:28:27:71:522:322:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:more widespread.  A survey of elementary school teachers in two large school districts asked 
4:83:5955:27:28:71:539:295:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:teachers to what extent they believed an array of questionable actions were practiced by teachers 
4:84:6053:28:28:71:533:267:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:in their school.  Almost ten percent of the teachers responded that they believed that teachers in 
4:85:6152:28:27:71:527:239:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:their school “often” or “frequently” give students answers to test questions.  Six percent of the 
4:86:6250:27:28:71:508:212:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:respondents believed that teachers “often” or “frequently” changed answers on a student’s 
4:87:6340:28:0:71:526:184:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:answer sheet (Shephard and Doughtery 1991).  In another study, 35 percent of North Carolina 
5:88:6433:0:27:71:538:708:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:teachers in grades 3, 6, 8 and 10 reported having witnessed cheating, including giving extra time 
5:89:6531:27:28:71:506:681:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:on tests, changing students’ answers, suggesting answers to students and directly teaching 
5:90:6622:28:27:71:227:653:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:sections of the test (Gay 1990).  
5:91:6656:27:28:107:532:626:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:A:Nonetheless, there has been very little previous empirical analysis of teacher cheating.3  
5:92:6747:28:28:71:498:598:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:The few studies that do exist involve investigations of specific instances of cheating and 
5:93:6838:28:27:71:542:570:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:generally rely on the analysis of erasure patterns and the controlled re-testing of students.4  While 
5:94:6940:27:28:71:510:543:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:this earlier research provides convincing evidence of isolated cheating incidents, our paper 
5:95:7033:28:27:71:529:515:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:represents the first systematic attempt to (1) identify the overall prevalence of teacher cheating 
5:96:7132:27:28:71:537:488:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:empirically and (2) analyze the factors that predict cheating.  To address these questions, we use 
5:97:7231:28:27:71:535:460:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:detailed administrative data from the Chicago Public Schools (CPS).  In particular, for the years 
5:98:7329:27:28:71:513:433:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:1993-2000, we have the question-by-question answers given by every student in grades 3-7 
5:99:7418:28:28:71:529:405:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:taking the Iowa Test of Basic Skills (ITBS).5  This test is administered annually to virtually all 
5:100:7517:28:48:71:533:377:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:elementary school students in the CPS.  In addition to the test responses, we also have access to 
5:101:7615:48:7:215:218:329:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:!:                                                           
5:102:7673:55:16:71:531:322:42:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:3  In contrast, there is a well-developed statistics literature for identifying whether one student has copied answers 
5:103:7792:16:11:71:527:306:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:from another student (Wollack 1997; Holland 1996; Frary 1993; Bellezza and Bellezza 1989; Fray, Tideman and 
5:104:7900:11:12:71:527:295:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:Watts 1977; Angoff 1974).  These methods involve the identification of unusual patterns of agreement in student 
5:105:8012:12:11:71:536:283:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:responses and, for the most part, are only effective in identifying the most egregious cases of copying.  Educational 
5:106:8130:11:12:71:534:272:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:Testing Services (ETS), the company that administers national tests such as the SAT, LSAT, and GRE, has funded 
5:107:8241:12:9:71:223:260:100:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:*:much of this research (Cizek 1999).   
5:108:8278:9:16:71:518:251:42:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:4 .  In the mid-eighties, Perlman (1985) investigated suspected cheating in a number of Chicago public schools 
5:109:8388:16:12:71:527:235:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:(CPS).  The study included 23 suspect schools— identified on the basis of a high percentage of erasures, unusual 
5:110:8501:12:11:71:516:223:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:patterns of score increases, unnecessarily large orders of blank answer sheets for the ITBS and tips to the CPS 
5:111:8613:11:12:71:541:212:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:Office of Research—along with 17 comparison schools.   When a second form of the test was administered to the 40 
5:112:8726:12:11:71:525:200:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:schools under more controlled conditions, the suspect schools did much worse than the comparison schools.  An 
5:113:8836:11:12:71:504:189:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:analysis of several dozen Los Angeles schools where the percentage of erasures and changed answers were 
5:114:8940:12:11:71:524:177:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:unusually high revealed evidence of teacher cheating (Aiken 1991).  One of the most highly publicized cheating 
5:115:9051:11:12:71:537:166:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:scandals involved Stratfield elementary, an award-winning school in Connecticut.  In 1996, the firm that developed 
5:116:9166:12:11:71:542:154:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:and scored the exam found that the rate of erasures at Stratfield was up to five times greater than other schools in the 
5:117:9287:11:12:71:533:143:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:same district and that 89 percent of erasures at Stratfield were from an incorrect to a correct response.  Subsequent 
5:118:9405:12:7:71:328:131:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:re-testing resulted in significantly lower scores (Lindsay 1996). 
5:119:9471:7:16:71:538:124:42:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:5  We do not, however, have access to the actual test forms that students filled out so we are unable to analyze these 
5:120:9590:16:0:71:283:108:100:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:*:tests for evidence of suspicious patterns of erasures. 
6:121:9645:0:27:71:527:708:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:each student’s full academic record, including past test scores, the school and room to which a 
6:122:9741:27:28:71:511:681:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:student was assigned, special education status, free-lunch eligibility, race, gender, and age. 
6:123:9836:28:27:107:529:653:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:A:Our approach to detecting classroom cheating uses two types of indicators: unexpected 
6:124:9922:27:28:71:536:626:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:test score fluctuations and unusual patterns of answers for students within a classroom.  Teacher 
6:125:10020:28:28:71:532:598:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:cheating increases the likelihood that students in a classroom will experience large, unexpected 
6:126:10117:28:27:71:528:570:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:increases in test scores one year, followed by very small test score gains (or even declines) the 
6:127:10215:27:28:71:537:543:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:following year.  Teacher cheating, especially if done in an unsophisticated manner, is also likely 
6:128:10314:28:27:71:539:515:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:to leave tell-tale signs in the form of blocks of identical answers, unusual patterns of correlations 
6:129:10416:27:28:71:518:488:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:across student answers within the classroom, or unusual response patterns within a student’s 
6:130:10509:28:27:71:530:460:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:exam (e.g., a student who answers a number of very difficult questions correctly while missing 
6:131:10604:27:28:71:197:433:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:many simple questions).   
6:132:10630:28:28:107:523:405:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:A:Empirically, however, not every classroom with test score fluctuations and suspicious 
6:133:10716:28:27:71:536:377:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:answer strings is cheating.  Sometimes such patterns arise by chance.  To identify the number of 
6:134:10813:27:28:71:485:350:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:cheating classrooms, we would like to compare the observed distribution of test score 
6:135:10899:28:27:71:513:322:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:fluctuations and suspicious answer strings to a counterfactual in which no cheating occurs.  
6:136:10992:27:28:71:510:295:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:Because we do not have the luxury of observing this counterfactual, we must instead make 
6:137:11081:28:28:71:540:267:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:assumptions about what the patterns would look like absent cheating.  Our identification strategy 
6:138:11179:28:27:71:540:239:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:hinges on three key assumptions: (1) cheating increases the likelihood a class will have large test 
6:139:11279:27:28:71:542:212:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:score fluctuations and suspicious answer strings, (2) if cheating classrooms had not cheated, their 
6:140:11379:28:27:71:513:184:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:distribution of test score fluctuations and answer strings would be identical to non-cheating 
6:141:11473:27:28:71:542:157:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:classrooms, and (3) the same pattern of correlation between test score fluctuations and suspicious 
6:142:11572:28:0:71:536:129:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:answers observed for non-cheating classrooms in other parts of the distribution also holds in the 
7:143:11671:0:27:71:521:708:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:upper tail of the distribution.  If these assumptions hold, then we can use part of the observed 
7:144:11768:27:28:71:538:681:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:distribution of outcomes that is unlikely to contain many cheaters (e.g. the 50th-75th percentile of 
7:145:11869:28:27:71:524:653:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:suspicious answer strings) to determine the natural correlation between test score fluctuations 
7:146:11965:27:28:71:538:626:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:and suspicious answer strings in non-cheating classrooms.  That allows us to predict the patterns 
7:147:12063:28:28:71:508:598:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:we would expect to observe in the tail of the distribution if no cheating occurred.  The gap 
7:148:12156:28:27:71:524:570:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:between the predicted and observed frequency of classrooms that are extreme on both the test 
7:149:12249:27:28:71:512:543:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:score fluctuation and suspicious answer string measures provides our estimate of cheating.  
7:150:12341:28:27:71:536:515:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:Because this identification strategy is necessarily indirect, we devote a great deal of space in the 
7:151:12442:27:28:71:539:488:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:paper to presenting a wide variety of tests attempting to confirm the validity of our approach, the 
7:152:12542:28:27:71:496:460:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:sensitivity of the results to alternative assumptions, and the plausibility of our findings.  
7:153:12636:27:28:107:536:433:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:A:Figure 1 provides a simple visual means of demonstrating the empirical approach taken.  
7:154:12724:28:28:71:515:405:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:The horizontal axis in the figure ranks classrooms according to how suspicious their answer 
7:155:12816:28:27:71:524:377:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:strings are according to our measures.6  The vertical axis is the fraction of the classrooms that 
7:156:12914:27:28:71:523:350:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:have unusually large test score increases one year followed by especially small gains the next 
7:157:13009:28:27:71:512:322:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:year.  The graph combines all classrooms and all subjects in our data.7  Consistent with our 
7:158:13102:27:28:71:528:295:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:assumptions, for most of the range, there is virtually no relationship between how suspicious a 
7:159:13198:28:28:71:501:267:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:classroom’s answer strings are and the likelihood of large test score fluctuations.  As one 
7:160:13290:28:27:71:527:239:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:approaches the extreme right tail of the distribution of suspicious answer strings, however, the 
7:161:13387:27:28:71:534:212:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:probability of large test score fluctuations rises dramatically, consistent with our conjecture that 
7:162:13488:28:30:71:532:184:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:cheating classrooms should be extreme on both of our measures.  To estimate the prevalence of 
7:163:13582:30:7:215:218:154:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:!:                                                           
7:164:13640:37:11:71:438:147:42:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:6  We defer a precise discussion of how we construct our cheating indicators to Section III. 
7:165:13733:11:16:71:532:136:42:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:7 To construct the figure, classes were rank ordered according to their answer strings and divided into 200 equally-
7:166:13849:16:12:71:526:120:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:sized segments.   The circles in the figure represent these 200 local means.  The line displayed in the graph is the 
7:167:13966:12:0:71:544:108:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:fitted value of a regression with a seventh-order polynomial in a classroom’s rank on the suspicious strings measure.  
8:168:14086:0:27:71:543:708:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:cheating, we will essentially compare the actual area under the curve in the far right tail of Figure 
8:169:14188:27:28:71:531:681:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:1 to the predicted area under the curve in the right tail under our maintained assumptions about 
8:170:14285:28:27:71:524:653:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:how the two measures co-vary in non-cheating classrooms.  Essentially, we predict that in the 
8:171:14379:27:28:71:513:626:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:absence of cheating, the relationship between suspicious answer strings and large test score 
8:172:14472:28:28:71:517:598:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:fluctuations would be linear over the entire range, rather than rising sharply in the tail.  That 
8:173:14570:28:27:71:317:570:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:sharp rise, we argue, is a consequence of cheating. 
8:174:14622:27:28:107:531:543:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:A: Empirically, we find evidence of cheating in approximately 200 classrooms per year in 
8:175:14709:28:27:71:540:515:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:our data, or four to five percent of the classes in our sample.  This estimate is likely to be a lower 
8:176:14812:27:28:71:505:488:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:bound on the true incidence of cheating for two reasons.  First, we focus only on the most 
8:177:14903:28:27:71:534:460:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:egregious type of cheating, where teachers systematically altering student test forms.  There are 
8:178:15001:27:28:71:542:433:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:other more subtle ways in which teachers can cheat, such as providing extra time to students, that 
8:179:15100:28:28:71:523:405:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:our algorithm is unlikely to detect.  Second, even when test forms are altered, our approach is 
8:180:15196:28:27:71:529:377:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:only partially successful in detecting illicit behavior.  When we ourselves simulate cheating by 
8:181:15293:27:28:71:506:350:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:altering student answer strings and then testing for cheating in the artificially manipulated 
8:182:15387:28:27:71:522:322:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:classrooms, many instances of moderate cheating go undetected.  This is particularly true if a 
8:183:15482:27:28:71:537:295:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:teacher employs a limited amount of sophistication in the cheating (e.g. avoiding changing large 
8:184:15579:28:28:71:322:267:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:blocks of consecutive questions for many students). 
8:185:15631:28:27:107:516:239:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:A:A number of patterns in the results reinforce our confidence that what we measure is 
8:186:15716:27:28:71:505:212:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:indeed cheating.  First, cheating on one part of the test (e.g., math) is a strong predictor of 
8:187:15812:28:27:71:507:184:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:cheating on other sections of the test (e.g., reading).  Second, cheating is correlated within 
8:188:15907:27:28:71:501:157:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:classrooms over time and across classrooms in a particular school.  Third, the students in 
8:189:15998:28:0:71:526:129:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:classrooms with large test score gains that are most likely attributable to cheating lose most of 
9:190:16097:0:27:71:540:708:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:their gains the following year.  In contrast, students in classrooms with large test-score gains that 
9:191:16199:27:28:71:518:681:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:do not have suspicious answer string patterns retain the majority of their gains the next year, 
9:192:16295:28:27:71:520:653:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:despite some loss due to mean reversion.  Fourth, simulation results demonstrate that there is 
9:193:16390:27:28:71:531:626:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:nothing mechanical about our identification approach that automatically generates patterns like 
9:194:16486:28:28:71:517:598:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:those observed in the data.  When we randomly assign students to classrooms and search for 
9:195:16577:28:27:71:540:570:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:cheating in these simulated classes, our methods find little evidence of cheating.  Finally, there is 
9:196:16679:27:28:71:541:543:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:no evidence that we are mistaking teachers focusing effort on specific subject areas (e.g. algebra, 
9:197:16779:28:27:71:517:515:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:fractions) for cheating.  The classrooms we label as cheaters are no more likely to have their 
9:198:16874:27:28:71:419:488:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:most suspicious answers cluster within a single topic than other classes. 
9:199:16948:28:27:107:537:460:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:A: In addition, the prevalence of cheating appears to respond to relatively minor changes in 
9:200:17039:27:28:71:542:433:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:teacher incentives.  The importance of standardized tests in the Chicago Public Schools increased 
9:201:17137:28:28:71:537:405:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:substantially with a change in leadership in 1996.  Schools that scored low on reading tests were 
9:202:17235:28:27:71:523:377:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:placed on probation and faced the threat of reconstitution (although no elementary school has 
9:203:17329:27:28:71:504:350:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:actually been reconstituted).  In addition, students in certain grades were required to meet 
9:204:17422:28:27:71:493:322:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:minimum test scores cutoffs in math and reading in order to advance to the next grade.  
9:205:17510:27:28:71:490:295:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:Following the introduction of these policies, the prevalence of cheating rose sharply in 
9:206:17599:28:28:71:534:267:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:classrooms with large numbers of low-achieving students.  In contrast, classrooms with average 
9:207:17694:28:27:71:509:239:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:or higher-achieving students showed no increase in cheating.  Finally, cheating prevalence 
9:208:17785:27:28:71:536:212:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:appears to be systematically lower in cases where the costs of cheating are higher (e.g. in mixed-
9:209:17883:28:27:71:535:184:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:grade classrooms in which two different exams are administered simultaneously) or the benefits 
9:210:17978:27:28:71:532:157:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:of cheating are lower (e.g. in classrooms with more special education or bilingual students who 
9:211:18074:28:0:71:483:129:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:take the standardized tests, but whose scores are excluded from official calculations). 
10:212:18163:0:27:107:497:708:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:A:The remainder of the paper is structured as follows.  Section II presents a simple 
10:213:18246:27:28:71:529:681:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:statistical model for detecting teacher cheating.  Section III introduces the particular indicators 
10:214:18346:28:27:71:491:653:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:we employ for detecting teacher cheating.  Section IV provides a brief overview of the 
10:215:18433:27:28:71:506:626:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:institutional details of the Chicago Public Schools and the data set that we use.  Section V 
10:216:18526:28:28:71:540:598:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:presents the basic empirical results on the prevalence of cheating.  Section VI analyzes in greater 
10:217:18626:28:27:71:541:570:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:detail the factors that influence which teachers cheat and for which students within the classroom 
10:218:18725:27:28:71:535:543:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:the teachers cheat.  Section VII discusses the results and the implications for increasing reliance 
10:219:18825:28:27:71:537:515:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:on high-stakes testing.  The appendix provides precise details of the construction of the cheating 
10:220:18924:27:28:71:221:488:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:indicators used in the analysis. 
10:221:18957:28:28:107:110:460:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:!: 
10:222:18957:56:27:71:296:432:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f3:C:II. A Statistical Model of Teacher Cheating 
10:223:19000:27:28:107:520:405:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:A:Assume that we have two measures of a classroom’s outcome on a standardized test.  
10:224:19083:28:27:71:517:377:144:g_d0_f5:144:g_d0_f3:B:SCOREc captures how well class c scores on the test, relative to how the same students have 
10:225:19175:27:28:71:528:350:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:done on past standardized tests and will do on future tests.  ANSWERSc measures how unusual 
10:226:19267:28:27:71:507:322:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:are the pattern of answers given by students in class c (e.g. are there are unusual blocks of 
10:227:19361:27:28:71:535:295:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:answers, or an especially high degree of correlation across student responses).  For simplicity in 
10:228:19460:28:28:71:536:267:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:presenting the model, we assume that these two measures take on one of two values:  SCOREc = 
10:229:19552:28:27:71:498:239:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:{low,high} and ANSWERSc = {typical,unusual}.  Further suppose there are two types of 
10:230:19636:27:28:71:518:212:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:classrooms:  those in which teachers cheat, and those in which they do not.  Define CHEATc 
10:231:19726:28:27:71:529:184:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:equal to one if cheating occurs, and zero otherwise.  Our first critical assumption is as follows: 
10:232:19825:27:28:71:524:157:144:g_d0_f5:144:g_d0_f5:C:(A1)     Had cheating classrooms not cheated, their distribution of the two outcome measures, 
10:233:19919:28:0:107:491:129:144:g_d0_f5:144:g_d0_f3:A:SCORE and ANSWERS, would be identical to that of non-cheating classrooms.

11:288:21160:27:28:71:532:162:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:strings provides an upper bound on the probability that non-cheating classrooms will have high

12:290:21353:0:27:71:521:708:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:test score fluctuations is an upper bound on the probability that non-cheating classrooms will 
12:291:21448:27:28:71:499:681:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:have unusual answer strings.  The reason these values are upper bounds is because some 
12:292:21535:28:27:71:533:653:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:classrooms that have “low” test scores or “typical” answer strings may actually be cheaters that 
12:293:21632:27:28:71:498:626:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:our methods fail to detect.  Even if cheaters are low on one dimension (either SCORE or 
12:294:21720:28:28:71:540:598:144:g_d0_f5:144:g_d0_f3:B:ANSWERS), they are still more likely to be elevated on the other measure, leading us to overstate 
12:295:21818:28:27:71:526:570:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:the baseline rate of high test scores or unusual answer strings among non-cheaters.  Only if all 
12:296:21915:27:28:71:536:543:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:cheating classrooms have high test scores and unusual answer strings will the bounds in Lemma 
12:297:22009:28:27:71:124:515:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:C:1 be strict. 
12:298:22022:27:28:107:538:488:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:A:Denote the total number of classrooms as N and the total number of classrooms that have 
12:299:22110:28:27:71:532:460:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:both high test scores and unusual answer strings as Nhu.  Then, a lower bound on the number of 
12:300:22205:27:28:71:527:433:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:cheating classrooms is how many extra rooms there are with both high test scores and unusual 
12:301:22298:28:30:71:494:405:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:answer strings, relative to the number that would be expected if no classrooms cheated: 
12:302:22386:30:40:107:286:375:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:C:(1) )(ˆncnchucheatASNNN××−= 
12:303:22413:40:28:82:523:335:49:g_d0_f5:144:g_d0_f3:*:cheatNˆ represents a lower bound on the number of cheating classrooms for two reasons.  First, 
12:304:22508:28:27:71:540:307:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:some cheating classrooms will not be detected by our measures and so will not register as having 
12:305:22605:27:28:71:542:280:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:high test scores and unusual strings.  Second, by Lemma 1, the probabilities of high test scores or 
12:306:22705:28:28:71:501:252:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:unusual answer strings among non-cheating classes are upper bounds on the true values.  
12:307:22793:28:27:107:537:224:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:A:Calculations like those in equation (1) provide the basis for our estimation of the number 
12:308:22884:27:28:71:520:197:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:of cheating classrooms.  In our empirical work, we generalize (1) by allowing for correlation 
12:309:22978:28:27:71:507:169:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:between test scores and answer string patterns in non-cheating classrooms, but the logic is 
12:310:23070:27:0:71:129:142:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:unchanged. 
13:311:23083:0:27:107:511:708:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:A:One important caveat to note is that we cannot identify any individual classroom as 
13:312:23167:27:28:71:532:681:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:cheating or not cheating with perfect certainty.  The probability that a class with high test score 
13:313:23267:28:36:71:380:653:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:fluctuations and unusual answer strings is cheating is given by:  
13:314:23333:36:13:107:124:617:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:C:(2) 
13:315:23337:13:0:450:457:604:49:g_d0_f5:49:g_d0_f5:D:hu
13:316:23339:0:8:445:460:622:49:g_d0_f5:49:g_d0_f5:D:cheat
13:317:23344:8:7:362:201:614:49:g_d0_f5:49:g_d0_f5:D:ccc
13:318:23347:7:0:440:448:607:144:g_d0_f5:144:g_d0_f5:D:N
13:319:23348:0:8:436:444:625:144:g_d0_f5:144:g_d0_f5:C:N
13:320:23349:8:40:379:468:617:144:g_d0_f5:144:g_d0_f3:D:unusualANSWERShighSCORECHEAT====),|1Pr( 
13:321:23388:40:29:71:515:577:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:A:As the thresholds for what constitutes a “high” test score or an “unusual” answer strings are 
13:322:23482:29:30:71:519:548:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:made more stringent, huNwill decline and, consequently, our level of certainty rises that any 
13:323:23576:30:28:71:506:518:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:particular classroom exhibiting these characteristics is cheating.   In essence, raising these 
13:324:23671:28:28:71:413:490:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:thresholds will decrease the number of false positives in our estimates. 
13:325:23744:28:27:107:110:462:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:!: 
13:326:23744:55:28:71:255:435:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f3:C:III. Indicators of Teacher Cheating 
13:327:23779:28:27:107:538:407:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:A:We employ two types of measures to detect cheating.  One indicator captures predictable 
13:328:23867:27:28:71:514:380:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:fluctuations in test scores that are likely to be associated with cheating.  The other indicator 
13:329:23964:28:28:71:531:352:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:summarizes the extent to which answer strings in a classroom appear unusual or suspicious.  In 
13:330:24059:28:27:71:527:324:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:this section, we discuss informally the indicators we use to detect cheating, and then provide a 
13:331:24156:27:28:71:534:297:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:concrete example that compares data from two actual classrooms: one in which there appears to 
13:332:24250:28:27:71:530:269:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:be cheating and one in which there does not.  Readers interested in a more rigorous description 
13:333:24346:27:28:71:395:242:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:of how the indicators are constructed are directed to the Appendix. 
13:334:24414:28:28:107:532:214:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:A:In selecting our measures of cheating, we focus on detecting teacher actions that lead to 
13:335:24504:28:32:71:532:186:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:large, artificial increases in test scores for a large number of students in the class.8  By focusing 
13:336:24606:32:7:215:218:154:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:!:                                                           
13:337:24664:39:16:71:523:147:42:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:8We have no way of knowing whether the patterns we observe arise because a teacher explicitly alters students’ 
13:338:24775:16:11:71:515:131:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:answer sheets, directly provides answers to students during a test, or perhaps makes test materials available to 
13:339:24888:11:12:71:538:120:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:students in advance of the exam (for instance, by teaching a reading passage that is on the test).  If we had access to 
13:340:25008:12:0:71:540:108:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:the actual exams, it might be possible to distinguish between these scenarios through an analysis of erasure patterns. 
14:341:25129:0:27:71:539:708:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:on the entire classroom, we are unlikely to misclassify cheating by individual students as teacher 
14:342:25228:27:28:71:506:681:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:cheating.  Teacher actions such as “teaching to the test” or allowing students extra time to 
14:343:25321:28:27:71:507:653:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:complete exams are not likely to be detected by our measures because they are unlikely to 
14:344:25411:27:28:71:405:626:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f4:B:generate sufficiently unusual response patterns in the answer strings. 
14:345:25481:28:28:107:528:598:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:A:Within this already restrictive definition of teacher cheating, we narrow our focus even 
14:346:25570:28:27:71:531:570:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:further by excluding a particular form of cheating that appears to be quite prevalent in the data: 
14:347:25669:27:28:71:527:543:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:teachers randomly filling in answers left blank by students.  For example, in some classrooms, 
14:348:25764:28:27:71:537:515:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:almost every student will end the test with a long string of “B’s” or an alternating pattern of “B” 
14:349:25864:27:28:71:527:488:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:and “C.”   The fact that almost all students in the class coordinate on the same pattern strongly 
14:350:25962:28:27:71:541:460:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:suggests that the students themselves did not fill in the blanks, or were under explicit instructions 
14:351:26064:27:28:71:524:433:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:by the teacher to do so.  Since there is no penalty for guessing on the test, filling in the blanks 
14:352:26164:28:28:71:532:405:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:can only increase student test scores.  While this type of teacher behavior is likely to be viewed 
14:353:26263:28:27:71:521:377:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:by many as unethical, we do not make it the focus of our analysis because (1) it is difficult to 
14:354:26360:27:28:71:516:350:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:provide definitive evidence of such behavior (a teacher could argue that he or she instructed 
14:355:26454:28:27:71:521:322:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:students well in advance of the test to fill in all blanks with the letter “C” as part of good test-
14:356:26554:27:28:71:477:295:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:taking strategy), and (2) in our minds it is categorically different than a teacher who 
14:357:26642:28:28:71:378:267:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:systematically changes student responses to the correct answer. 
14:358:26706:28:27:71:74:239:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:!: 
14:359:26706:55:28:71:362:212:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:C:Cheating Indicator #1: Unexpected Test Score Fluctuations  
14:360:26764:28:27:107:520:184:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:A:Given that the aim of cheating is to raise test scores, an obvious potential indicator of 
14:361:26854:27:28:71:531:157:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:teacher cheating is a classroom that experiences unexpectedly large gains in test scores relative 
14:362:26952:28:0:71:524:129:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:to how those same students tested in the previous year.  Since test score gains that result from 
15:363:27051:0:27:71:523:708:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:cheating do not represent real gains in knowledge, there is no reason to expect the gains to be 
15:364:27147:27:28:71:525:681:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:sustained on future exams taken by these students (unless, of course, next year’s teachers also 
15:365:27243:28:27:71:541:653:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:cheat on behalf of the students).  Thus, large gains due to cheating should be followed by smaller 
15:366:27342:27:28:71:539:626:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:than usual test score gains for these students in the following year.  In contrast, if large test score 
15:367:27446:28:28:71:508:598:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:gains are due to a talented teacher, the student gains are likely to have a greater permanent 
15:368:27540:28:27:71:343:570:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:component, even if some regression to the mean occurs. 
15:369:27595:27:28:107:531:543:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:A:In practice, the choice of a cutoff for what represents an “unexpectedly” large test score 
15:370:27686:28:27:71:533:515:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:gain or loss is somewhat arbitrary.  Our admittedly simple approach is to rank each classroom’s 
15:371:27782:27:28:71:524:488:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:average test score gains relative to all other classrooms in that same subject, grade, and year,9 
15:372:27879:28:29:71:249:460:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:and construct the following statistic: 
15:373:27918:29:32:107:417:431:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:A:(3) 21,,2,,)_1()_(+−+=tbctbccbtgainrankgainrankSCORE 
15:374:27970:32:28:71:540:399:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:where rank_gaincbt is the percentile rank for class c in subject b in year t.  Classes with relatively 
15:375:28073:28:27:71:541:371:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:big gains on this year’s test and relatively small gains on next year’s test will have high values of 
15:376:28175:27:28:71:538:344:144:g_d0_f5:144:g_d0_f3:B:SCORE.  Squaring the individual terms gives more relatively more weight to big test score gains 
15:377:28271:28:28:71:534:316:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:this year and big test score declines the following year.10  In the empirical analysis, we consider 
15:378:28371:28:27:71:535:288:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:three possible cutoffs for what it means to have a “high” value on SCORE, corresponding to the 
15:379:28466:27:28:71:394:261:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:80th, 90th, and 95th percentiles among all classrooms in the sample. 
15:380:28535:28:33:107:110:233:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:!: 
15:381:28535:61:7:215:218:200:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:!:                                                           
15:382:28593:68:16:71:513:193:42:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:9 We also experimented with more complicated mechanisms for defining large or small test score gains  (e.g., 
15:383:28702:16:11:71:542:177:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:predicting each student’s expected test score gain as a function of past test scores and background characteristics and 
15:384:28822:11:12:71:538:166:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:computing a deviation measure for each student which was then aggregated to the classroom level), but because the 
15:385:28936:12:11:71:537:154:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:results were similar we elected to use the simpler method.  We have also defined gains and losses using an absolute 
15:386:29052:11:12:71:504:143:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:metric (e.g., where gains in excess of 1.5 or 2 grade equivalents are considered unusually large), and obtain 
15:387:29162:12:7:71:151:131:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:comparable results. 
15:388:29182:7:16:71:517:124:42:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:10  In the following year the students who were in a particular classroom are typically scattered across multiple 
15:389:29296:16:0:71:394:108:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:classrooms.  We base all calculations off of the composition of this year’s class. 
16:390:29381:0:27:71:318:708:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:Cheating Indicator #2:  Suspicious Answer Strings 
16:391:29430:27:28:107:533:681:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:A:Teacher cheating, particularly if accomplished by the teacher actually changing answers 
16:392:29518:28:27:71:522:653:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:on test forms, is likely to leave a discernible trail in student answer strings.  The quickest and 
16:393:29617:27:28:71:502:626:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:easiest way for a teacher to cheat is to alter the same block of consecutive questions for a 
16:394:29710:28:28:71:512:598:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:substantial portion of students in the class.  More sophisticated interventions might involve 
16:395:29804:28:27:71:526:570:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:skipping some questions so as to avoid a large block of identical answers, or altering different 
16:396:29901:27:28:71:279:543:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:blocks of questions for different students.   
16:397:29947:28:27:107:535:515:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:A:We combine four different measures of how suspicious a classroom’s answer strings are 
16:398:30033:27:28:71:519:488:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:in determining whether a classroom may be cheating.  The first measure focuses on the most 
16:399:30124:28:27:71:534:460:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:unlikely block of identical answers given by students on consecutive questions.  Using past test 
16:400:30221:27:28:71:518:433:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:scores, future test scores, and background characteristics, we predict the likelihood that each 
16:401:30317:28:28:71:519:405:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:student will give each possible answer (A, B, C or D) on every question using a multinomial 
16:402:30409:28:27:71:529:377:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:logit.  This means that each student’s predicted probability of choosing a particular response is 
16:403:30507:27:28:71:536:350:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:identified by the likelihood that other students (in the same year, grade and subject) with similar 
16:404:30607:28:27:71:530:322:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:background characteristics will choose that response.  We then search over all combinations of 
16:405:30702:27:28:71:531:295:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:students and consecutive questions to find the block of identical answers given by students in a 
16:406:30799:28:28:71:542:267:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:classroom least likely to have arisen by chance.11  The more unusual is the most unusual block of 
16:407:30897:28:27:71:530:239:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:test responses (adjusting for class size and the number of questions on the exam, both of which 
16:408:30993:27:35:71:517:212:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:increase the possible combinations over which we search), the more likely it is that cheating 
16:409:31087:35:7:215:218:177:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:!:                                                           
16:410:31145:42:16:71:520:170:42:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:11  Note that we do not require the answers to be correct.  Indeed, in many classrooms, the most unusual strings 
16:411:31258:16:11:71:511:154:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:include some incorrect answers.  Note also that these calculations are done under the assumption that a given 
16:412:31368:11:12:71:535:143:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:student’s answers are uncorrelated (conditional on observables) across questions on the exam, and that answers are 
16:413:31483:12:11:71:540:131:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:uncorrelated across students.  Of course, this assumption is unlikely to be true.  Since all of our comparisons rely on 
16:414:31603:11:12:71:541:120:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:the relative unusualness of the answers given in different classrooms, this simplifying assumption is not problematic 
16:415:31721:12:0:71:352:108:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:D:unless the correlation within and across students varies by classroom. 
17:416:31794:0:27:71:540:708:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:occurred.  Thus, if ten very bright students in a class of thirty give the correct answers to the first 
17:417:31898:27:28:71:542:681:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:five questions on the exam (typically the easier questions), the block of identical answers will not 
17:418:31999:28:27:71:525:653:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:appear unusual.  In contrast, if all fifteen students in a low-achieving classroom give the same 
17:419:32096:27:28:71:540:626:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:correct answers to the last five questions on the exam (typically the harder questions), this would 
17:420:32196:28:28:71:174:598:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:appear quite suspect. 
17:421:32218:28:27:107:492:570:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:A:The second measure of suspicious answer strings involves the overall degree of 
17:422:32297:27:28:71:534:543:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:correlation in student answers across the test.  When a teacher changes answers on test forms, it 
17:423:32395:28:27:71:513:515:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:presumably increases the uniformity of student test forms across students in the class.  This 
17:424:32489:27:28:71:523:488:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:measure is meant to capture more general patterns of similarity in student responses that goes 
17:425:32584:28:27:71:535:460:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:beyond just identical blocks of answers.  Based on the results of the multinomial logit described 
17:426:32682:27:28:71:533:433:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:above, for each question and each student we create a measure of how unexpected the student’s 
17:427:32776:28:28:71:514:405:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:response was.  We then combine the information for each student in the classroom to create 
17:428:32867:28:27:71:528:377:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:something akin to the within-classroom correlation in student responses.  This measure will be 
17:429:32962:27:28:71:542:350:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:high if students in a classroom tend to give the same answers on many questions, especially if the 
17:430:33061:28:27:71:531:322:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:answers given are unexpected, i.e. correct answers on hard questions or systematic mistakes on 
17:431:33156:27:28:71:150:295:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:easy questions.  
17:432:33173:28:28:107:532:267:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:A:Of course, within-classroom correlation may arise for many reasons other than cheating 
17:433:33260:28:27:71:506:239:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:(e.g., the teacher may emphasize certain topics during the school year).  Therefore, a third 
17:434:33353:27:28:71:529:212:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:indicator of potential cheating is a high variance in the degree of correlation across questions.  
17:435:33452:28:27:71:529:184:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:That is, on some questions students’ answers are highly correlated, but on other questions they 
17:436:33548:27:28:71:526:157:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:are not.  If the teacher changes answers for multiple students on selected questions, the within-
17:437:33645:28:0:71:538:129:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:class correlation on those particular questions will be extremely high, while the degree of within-
18:438:33746:0:27:71:539:708:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:class correlation on other questions is likely to be typical.  This leads the cross-question variance 
18:439:33848:27:28:71:380:681:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:in correlations to be larger than normal in cheating classrooms.  
18:440:33914:28:27:107:542:653:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:A:Our final indicator compares the answers that students in one classroom give compared to 
18:441:34003:27:28:71:533:626:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:other students in the system who take the identical test and get the exact same score.  Questions 
18:442:34101:28:28:71:508:598:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:vary significantly in difficulty.  The typical student will answer most of the easy questions 
18:443:34195:28:27:71:532:570:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:correctly and get most of the hard questions wrong (where “easy” and “hard” are based on how 
18:444:34288:27:28:71:536:543:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:well students of similar ability do on the question).  If students in a class systematically miss the 
18:445:34390:28:27:71:502:515:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:easy questions while correctly answering the hard questions, this may be an indication of 
18:446:34480:27:28:71:118:488:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:cheating. 
18:447:34490:28:27:107:527:460:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:A:Our overall measure of suspicious answer strings is constructed in a manner parallel to 
18:448:34578:27:28:71:536:433:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:our measure of unusual test score fluctuations.  Within a given subject, grade, and year, we rank 
18:449:34676:28:28:71:525:405:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:classrooms on each of these four indicators, and then take the sum of squared ranks across the 
18:450:34771:28:29:71:156:377:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:B:four measures:12
